<p><a href="/wiki/John_Fitzgerald_Kennedy" class="mw-redirect" title="John Fitzgerald Kennedy">John Fitzgerald Kennedy</a>, the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Presidents of the United States">35th</a> <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a>, was <a href="/wiki/Assassination" title="Assassination">assassinated</a> on November 22, 1963, at 12:30&#160;p.m. <a href="/wiki/Central_Time_Zone" title="Central Time Zone">Central Standard Time</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dallas,_Texas" class="mw-redirect" title="Dallas, Texas">Dallas, Texas</a>, while riding in a presidential <a href="/wiki/Motorcade" title="Motorcade">motorcade</a> through <a href="/wiki/Dealey_Plaza" title="Dealey Plaza">Dealey Plaza</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStokes197921_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStokes197921-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy was riding with his wife <a href="/wiki/Jacqueline_Kennedy_Onassis" title="Jacqueline Kennedy Onassis">Jacqueline</a>, <a href="/wiki/Texas_Governor" class="mw-redirect" title="Texas Governor">Texas Governor</a> <a href="/wiki/John_Connally" title="John Connally">John Connally</a>, and Connally's wife <a href="/wiki/Nellie_Connally" title="Nellie Connally">Nellie</a> when he was fatally shot by former U.S. Marine <a href="/wiki/Lee_Harvey_Oswald" title="Lee Harvey Oswald">Lee Harvey Oswald</a> firing in ambush from a nearby building. Governor Connally was seriously wounded in the attack. The motorcade rushed to <a href="/wiki/Parkland_Memorial_Hospital" title="Parkland Memorial Hospital">Parkland Memorial Hospital</a> where President Kennedy was pronounced dead about 30 minutes after the shooting; Connally recovered from his injuries.
</p><p>Oswald was arrested by the <a href="/wiki/Dallas_Police_Department" title="Dallas Police Department">Dallas Police Department</a> 70 minutes after the initial shooting. Oswald was charged under Texas state law with the murder of Kennedy, as well as that of Dallas policeman <a href="/wiki/J._D._Tippit" title="J. D. Tippit">J. D. Tippit</a>, who had been fatally shot a short time after the assassination. At 11:21&#160;a.m. November 24, 1963, as live television cameras were covering his transfer from the city jail to the county jail, Oswald was fatally shot in the basement of Dallas Police Headquarters by Dallas nightclub operator <a href="/wiki/Jack_Ruby" title="Jack Ruby">Jack Ruby</a>. Oswald was taken to Parkland Memorial Hospital where he soon died. Ruby was convicted of Oswald's murder, though it was later overturned on appeal, and Ruby died in prison in 1967 while awaiting a new trial.
</p><p>After a 10-month investigation, the <a href="/wiki/Warren_Commission" title="Warren Commission">Warren Commission</a> concluded that Oswald assassinated Kennedy, that Oswald had acted entirely alone, and that Ruby had acted alone in killing Oswald.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy was the eighth and most recent US President to die in office, and the fourth (following <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Abraham_Lincoln" title="Assassination of Abraham Lincoln">Lincoln</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_James_A._Garfield" title="Assassination of James A. Garfield">Garfield</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_William_McKinley" title="Assassination of William McKinley">McKinley</a>) to be assassinated. Vice President <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">Lyndon B. Johnson</a> automatically assumed the Presidency upon Kennedy's death.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A later investigation, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Select_Committee_on_Assassinations" title="United States House Select Committee on Assassinations">United States House Select Committee on Assassinations</a> (HSCA) agreed with the Warren Commission that the injuries that Kennedy and Connally sustained were caused by Oswald's three rifle shots, but they also concluded that Kennedy was "probably assassinated as a result of a conspiracy"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStokes19792_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStokes19792-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> as analysis of a <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy_assassination_Dictabelt_recording" title="John F. Kennedy assassination Dictabelt recording">dictabelt audio recording</a> pointed to the existence of an additional gunshot and therefore "... a high probability that two gunmen fired at [the] President".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStokes197990–93_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStokes197990–93-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStokes197965_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStokes197965-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The Committee was not able to identify any individuals or groups involved with the possible conspiracy. In addition, the HSCA found that the original federal investigations were "seriously flawed" with respect to information-sharing and the possibility of conspiracy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStokes1979241-255_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStokes1979241-255-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> As recommended by the HSCA, the acoustic evidence indicating conspiracy was subsequently re-examined and rejected.<sup id="cite_ref-CBAReport_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CBAReport-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In light of the investigative reports determining that "reliable acoustic data do not support a conclusion that there was a second gunman", the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Justice_Department" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Justice Department">U.S. Justice Department</a> concluded active investigations and stated "that no persuasive evidence can be identified to support the theory of a conspiracy in ... the assassination of President Kennedy".<sup id="cite_ref-JusticeDeptMemo_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JusticeDeptMemo-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> However, Kennedy's assassination is still the subject of widespread debate and has spawned <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy_assassination_conspiracy_theories" title="John F. Kennedy assassination conspiracy theories">numerous conspiracy theories</a> and alternative scenarios. Polls conducted from 1966 to 2004 found that up to 80 percent of Americans suspected that there was a plot or <a href="/wiki/Cover-up" title="Cover-up">cover-up</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-langer20031116_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-langer20031116-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Timeline">Timeline</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_John_F._Kennedy_assassination" title="Timeline of the John F. Kennedy assassination">Timeline of the John F. Kennedy assassination</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span></h3>
<p>President John F. Kennedy chose to travel to Texas to smooth over frictions in the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party</a> between liberals <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Yarborough" title="Ralph Yarborough">Ralph Yarborough</a> and <a href="/wiki/Don_Yarborough" title="Don Yarborough">Don Yarborough</a> (no relation) and conservative <a href="/wiki/John_Connally" title="John Connally">John Connally</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A presidential visit to Texas was first agreed upon by Kennedy, Vice President <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">Lyndon B. Johnson</a> (a Texas native), and Texas <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Texas" title="Governor of Texas">Governor</a> <a href="/wiki/John_Connally" title="John Connally">John Connally</a> while all three men were together in a meeting in <a href="/wiki/El_Paso,_Texas" title="El Paso, Texas">El Paso</a> on June 5, 1963.<sup id="cite_ref-WarrenCommission-Page28_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WarrenCommission-Page28-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>President Kennedy later decided to embark on the trip with three basic goals in mind: 1.) to help raise more Democratic Party presidential campaign fund contributions;<sup id="cite_ref-WarrenCommission-Page28_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WarrenCommission-Page28-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> 2.) begin his quest for reelection in November 1964;<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> and 3.) to help mend political fences among several leading Texas Democratic party members who appeared to be fighting politically amongst themselves since the Kennedy-Johnson ticket had barely won Texas in 1960 (and had even lost in Dallas).<sup id="cite_ref-VisitPlanned_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VisitPlanned-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>President Kennedy's trip to Dallas was first announced to the public in September 1963.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The exact motorcade route was finalized on November 18 and publicly announced a few days before November 22.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Route_to_Dealey_Plaza">Route to Dealey Plaza</span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r889293853/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{text-align:left;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output motorcade</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:262px;max-width:262px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Altgens_mary_ferrell.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" (Secret Service)">Clint Hill</a> shields the occupants of the Presidential limousine moments after the fatal shots. Note the effect of panning in the photograph.</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:262px;max-width:262px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:HowardBrennan.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/HowardBrennan.jpg/260px-HowardBrennan.jpg" decoding="async" width="260" height="214" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/HowardBrennan.jpg/390px-HowardBrennan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/HowardBrennan.jpg/520px-HowardBrennan.jpg 2x" data-file-width="532" data-file-height="438" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Witness <a href="/wiki/Howard_Brennan" title="Howard Brennan">Howard Brennan</a> sitting in the identical spot across from the Texas School Book Depository four months after the assassination. Circle "A" indicates where he saw Oswald fire a rifle at the motorcade.</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:262px;max-width:262px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Dallasjfk.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Dallasjfk.jpg/260px-Dallasjfk.jpg" decoding="async" width="260" height="271" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Dallasjfk.jpg/390px-Dallasjfk.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Dallasjfk.jpg/520px-Dallasjfk.jpg 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="781" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">In this 2008, photo, arrows indicate the sixth-floor window of the <a href="/wiki/Texas_School_Book_Depository" title="Texas School Book Depository">Texas School Book Depository</a> and the spot on  Elm Street at which Kennedy was struck in the head. Right of the depository is the <a href="/wiki/Dal-Tex_Building" title="Dal-Tex Building">Dal-Tex Building</a>.</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Kennedy's motorcade route through Dallas with Johnson and Connally was planned to give the president maximum exposure to local crowds before his arrival<sup id="cite_ref-odonnell_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-odonnell-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> for a luncheon at the <a href="/wiki/Dallas_Market_Center" title="Dallas Market Center">Trade Mart</a>, where he would meet with civic and business leaders. The <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a> staff informed the Secret Service that the President would arrive at <a href="/wiki/Dallas_Love_Field" title="Dallas Love Field">Dallas Love Field</a> via a short flight from <a href="/wiki/Carswell_Air_Force_Base" title="Carswell Air Force Base">Carswell Air Force Base</a> in <a href="/wiki/Fort_Worth" class="mw-redirect" title="Fort Worth">Fort Worth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-odonnell_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-odonnell-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._2,_p._31_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._2,_p._31-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Dallas Trade Mart was preliminarily selected as the place for the luncheon, and <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_O%27Donnell" title="Kenneth O&#39;Donnell">Kenneth O'Donnell</a>, President Kennedy's friend and appointments secretary, had selected it as the final destination on the motorcade route.<sup id="cite_ref-odonnell_18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-odonnell-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._2,_p._31_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._2,_p._31-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Leaving from <a href="/wiki/Dallas_Love_Field" title="Dallas Love Field">Dallas Love Field</a>, the motorcade had been allotted 45&#160;minutes to reach the Trade Mart at a planned arrival time of 12:15&#160;p.m. The itinerary was designed to serve as a meandering 10-mile (16-km) route between the two places, and the motorcade vehicles could be driven slowly within the allotted time.
</p><p>Special Agent Winston G. Lawson, a member of the White House detail who acted as the advance Secret Service Agent, and Secret Service Agent Forrest V. Sorrels, Special Agent in charge of the Dallas office, were the most active in planning the actual motorcade route. On November 14, both men attended a meeting at Love Field and drove over the route that Sorrels believed was best suited for the motorcade. From Love Field, the route passed through a suburban section of Dallas, through Downtown along Main Street, and finally to the Trade Mart via a short segment of the <a href="/wiki/Stemmons_Freeway" class="mw-redirect" title="Stemmons Freeway">Stemmons Freeway</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._2,_p._40_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._2,_p._40-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The President had planned to return to Love Field to depart for a fundraising dinner in <a href="/wiki/Austin,_Texas" title="Austin, Texas">Austin</a> later that day. For the return trip, the agents selected a more direct route, which was approximately four miles, or 6.4 kilometers (some of this route would be used after the assassination). The planned route to the Trade Mart was widely reported in Dallas newspapers several days before the event, for the benefit of people who wished to view the motorcade.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._2,_p._40_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._2,_p._40-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>To pass directly through Downtown Dallas, a route west along Main Street, rather than Elm Street (one block to the north) was chosen, since this was the traditional parade route and provided the maximal building and crowd views. The Main Street section of the route precluded a direct turn onto the Fort Worth Turnpike exit (which served also as the Stemmons Freeway exit), which was the route to the Trade Mart, as this exit was only accessible from Elm Street. Therefore, the planned motorcade route included a short one-block turn at the end of the downtown segment of Main Street, onto Houston Street for one block northward, before turning again west onto Elm, that way they could proceed through <a href="/wiki/Dealey_Plaza" title="Dealey Plaza">Dealey Plaza</a> before exiting Elm onto the Stemmons Freeway. The <a href="/wiki/Texas_School_Book_Depository" title="Texas School Book Depository">Texas School Book Depository</a> was situated at the northwest corner of the Houston and Elm Street intersection.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Three vehicles were used for Secret Service and police protection in the Dallas motorcade. The first car, an unmarked white Ford (hardtop), carried Dallas Police Chief Jesse Curry, Secret Service Agent Win Lawson, Sheriff Bill Decker and Dallas Field Agent Forrest Sorrels. The second car, a 1961 Lincoln Continental convertible, was occupied by driver Agent Bill Greer, SAIC Roy Kellerman, Governor John Connally, Nellie Connally, President Kennedy, and Jackie Kennedy.<sup id="cite_ref-Blaine,_G._2003_p._196_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blaine,_G._2003_p._196-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The third car, a 1955 Cadillac convertible code-named "Halfback", contained driver Agent Sam Kinney, ATSAIC Emory Roberts, presidential aides Ken O'Donnell and Dave Powers, driver Agent George Hickey and PRS agent Glen Bennett. Secret Service agents Clint Hill, Jack Ready, Tim McIntyre and Paul Landis rode on the running boards.
</p><p>On November 22—after a breakfast speech in Fort Worth, where President Kennedy had stayed overnight after arriving from <a href="/wiki/San_Antonio" title="San Antonio">San Antonio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Houston" title="Houston">Houston</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a>, the previous day<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>—the president boarded <a href="/wiki/Air_Force_One" title="Air Force One">Air Force One</a>, which departed at 11:10 and arrived at Love Field 15 minutes later. At about 11:40, the presidential motorcade left Love Field for the trip through Dallas, running on a schedule about 10 minutes longer than the planned 45, due to enthusiastic crowds estimated at 150,000–200,000 people, and two unplanned stops directed by the president.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> By the time the motorcade reached Dealey Plaza, they were only five minutes away from their planned destination.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Assassination">Assassination</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Shooting_in_Dealey_Plaza">Shooting in Dealey Plaza</span></h4>
<p>President Kennedy's open-top 1961 Lincoln Continental four-door convertible <a href="/wiki/SS-100-X" title="SS-100-X">limousine</a> entered <a href="/wiki/Dealey_Plaza" title="Dealey Plaza">Dealey Plaza</a> at 12:30&#160;p.m. CST. Nellie Connally, the First Lady of Texas, turned around to the President, who was sitting behind her, and commented, "Mr. President, you can't say Dallas doesn't love you," which President Kennedy acknowledged by saying, "No, you certainly can't." Those were Kennedy's last words.<sup id="cite_ref-nellieconnally_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nellieconnally-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-johnconnally_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-johnconnally-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>From Houston Street, the presidential limousine made the planned left turn onto Elm, providing it access to the Stemmons Freeway exit. As the vehicle turned onto Elm, the motorcade passed by the <a href="/wiki/Texas_School_Book_Depository" title="Texas School Book Depository">Texas School Book Depository</a>. Suddenly, shots were fired at President Kennedy as his motorcade continued down Elm Street. About 80% of the witnesses recalled hearing three shots.<sup id="cite_ref-Earwitnesses_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Earwitnesses-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A minority of the witnesses recognized the first gunshot they heard as weapon fire, but there was hardly any reaction to the first shot from a majority of the people in the crowd or those riding in the motorcade. Many bystanders later said that they heard what they first thought to either be a firecracker or the backfire of one of the vehicles shortly after the President had begun waving.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Although some close witnesses<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> recalled seeing the limousine slow down, nearly stop, or completely stop, the Warren Commission—based on the <a href="/wiki/Zapruder_film" title="Zapruder film">Zapruder film</a>—found that the limousine had traveled an average speed of 11.2&#160;miles per hour over the 186&#160;ft of Elm Street immediately preceding the fatal head shot.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Within one second of each other, Governor Connally and Mrs. Kennedy turn abruptly from looking to their left to looking to their right, beginning at Zapruder film frame 162.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Connally, like the President, was a <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> military veteran but unlike him, a longtime hunter. Connally testified that he immediately recognized the sound as that of a high-powered rifle, then he turned his head and torso rightward, attempting to see President Kennedy behind him. Governor Connally testified he could not see the President, so he then started to turn forward again (turning from his right to his left). The governor also testified that when his head was facing about 20 degrees left of center,<sup id="cite_ref-johnconnally_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-johnconnally-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> he was hit in his upper right back by a bullet that he did not hear get fired. The doctor who operated on Connally measured his head at the time he was hit as having turned 27 degrees left of center.<sup id="cite_ref-johnconnally_27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-johnconnally-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> After Connally was hit, he shouted, "Oh, no, no, no. My God. They're going to kill us all!"<sup id="cite_ref-jacqueline_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jacqueline-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Mrs. Connally testified that just after hearing a loud, frightening noise that came from somewhere behind her and to her right, she turned toward President Kennedy and saw him raise up his arms and elbows, with his hands in front of his face and throat. She then heard another gunshot and then Governor Connally yelling. Mrs. Connally then turned away from Kennedy toward her husband, at which point another gunshot sounded, and both she and the limousine's rear interior were covered with fragments of skull, blood, and brain.
</p><p>According to the Warren Commission<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._1,_pp._18–19_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._1,_pp._18–19-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> and the House Select Committee on Assassinations,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStokes197941–46_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStokes197941–46-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy was waving to the crowds on his right with his right arm upraised on the side of the limo when a shot entered his upper back, penetrated his neck and slightly damaged a spinal vertebra and the top of his right lung. The bullet exited his throat nearly centerline just beneath his larynx and nicked the left side of his suit tie knot. He raised his elbows and clenched his fists in front of his face and neck, then leaned forward and left. Mrs. Kennedy, facing him, then put her arms around him in concern.<sup id="cite_ref-johnconnally_27-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-johnconnally-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-aarclibrary.org_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aarclibrary.org-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the Warren Commission's <a href="/wiki/Single-bullet_theory" title="Single-bullet theory">single bullet theory</a>, Governor Connally also reacted after the same bullet penetrated his back just below his right armpit. The bullet created an oval-shaped entry wound, impacted and destroyed four inches of his right fifth rib, and exited his chest just below his right nipple. This created a two-and-a-half inch oval-shaped air-sucking chest wound. That same bullet then entered his arm just above his right wrist and cleanly shattered his right radius bone into eight pieces. The bullet exited just below the wrist at the inner side of his right palm and finally lodged in his left inner thigh.<sup id="cite_ref-johnconnally_27-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-johnconnally-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-aarclibrary.org_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aarclibrary.org-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> The Warren Commission theorized that the "single bullet" struck sometime between Zapruder frames 210 and 225, while the House Select Committee theorized that it struck at approximately Zapruder frame 190.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the Warren Commission, a second shot that struck the President was recorded at Zapruder film frame 313. The Commission made no conclusion as to whether this was the second or third bullet fired. The presidential limousine then passed in front of the <a href="/wiki/John_Neely_Bryan" title="John Neely Bryan">John Neely Bryan</a> north <a href="/wiki/Pergola" title="Pergola">pergola</a> concrete structure. The two investigative committees concluded that the second shot to hit the president entered the rear of his head (the House Select Committee placed the entry wound four inches higher than the Warren Commission placed it) and passed in fragments through his skull; this created a large, "roughly ovular" [sic] hole on the rear, right side of the head. The president's blood and fragments of his scalp, brain, and skull landed on the interior of the car, the inner and outer surfaces of the front glass windshield, the raised sun visors, the front engine hood, and the rear trunk lid. His blood and fragments also landed on the Secret Service follow-up car and its driver's left arm, as well on the motorcycle officers who were riding on both sides of the President just behind his vehicle.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/United_States_Secret_Service" title="United States Secret Service">Secret Service</a> <a href="/wiki/Special_Agent" class="mw-redirect" title="Special Agent">Special Agent</a> <a href="/wiki/Clint_Hill_(Secret_Service)" title="Clint Hill (Secret Service)">Clint Hill</a> was riding on the left front <a href="/wiki/Running_board" title="Running board">running board</a> of the follow-up car, which was immediately behind the Presidential limousine. Hill testified that he heard one shot, then, as documented in other films and concurrent with Zapruder frame 308, he jumped off into Elm Street and ran forward to try to get on the limousine and protect the President; Hill testified to the Warren Commission that after he jumped into Elm Street, he heard two more shots.<sup id="cite_ref-cjhill_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cjhill-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After the President was shot in the head, Mrs. Kennedy began climbing out onto the back of the limousine, though she later didn't have any recollection of doing so.<sup id="cite_ref-jacqueline_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jacqueline-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Hill believed she was reaching for something, perhaps a piece of the President's skull.<sup id="cite_ref-cjhill_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cjhill-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> He jumped onto the back of the limousine while at the same time Mrs. Kennedy returned to her seat, and he clung to the car as it exited Dealey Plaza and accelerated, speeding to <a href="/wiki/Parkland_Memorial_Hospital" title="Parkland Memorial Hospital">Parkland Memorial Hospital</a>.
</p><p>After Mrs. Kennedy crawled back into her limousine seat, both Governor and Mrs. Connally heard her repeatedly say, "They have killed my husband. I have his brains in my hand."<sup id="cite_ref-nellieconnally_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nellieconnally-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-johnconnally_27-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-johnconnally-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Mrs. Kennedy recalled, "All the ride to the hospital I kept bending over him saying, 'Jack, Jack, can you hear me? I love you, Jack.' I kept holding the top of his head down trying to keep the brains in."<sup id="cite_ref-Leaming2014_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leaming2014-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Governor_Connally_and_a_spectator_wounded">Governor Connally and a spectator wounded</span></h4>
<p>Governor Connally was riding in the same limousine in a seat directly in front of the President and three inches more to the left than Kennedy; he was also seriously injured, but survived. Doctors later stated that after the Governor was shot, his wife pulled him onto her lap, and the resulting posture helped close his front chest wound, which was causing air to be sucked directly into his chest around his collapsed right lung.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/James_Tague" title="James Tague">James Tague</a> was a spectator and witness to the assassination. He received a minor wound to the right cheek while standing 531 feet (162&#160;m) away from the depository's sixth floor easternmost window, 270 feet (82&#160;m) in front of and slightly to the right of President Kennedy's head facing direction and more than 16 feet (4.9&#160;m) below the top of the President's head. Tague's injury occurred when a bullet or bullet fragment with no copper casing struck the nearby Main Street south curb. A deputy sheriff noticed some blood on Tague's cheek, and Tague realized that something had stung his face during the shooting. When Tague pointed to where he had been standing, the police officer noticed a bullet smear on a nearby curb. Nine months later the FBI removed the curb, and a spectrographic analysis revealed metallic residue consistent with that of the lead core in Oswald's ammunition.<sup id="cite_ref-newsweek_112814_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newsweek_112814-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Tague testified before the Warren Commission and initially stated that he was wounded on his cheek by either the second or third shot of the three shots that he remembered hearing. When the Commission counsel pressed him to be more specific, Tague testified that he was wounded by the second shot.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aftermath_in_Dealey_Plaza">Aftermath in Dealey Plaza</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Newman_Family.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Newman_Family.jpg/260px-Newman_Family.jpg" decoding="async" width="260" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Newman_Family.jpg/390px-Newman_Family.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Newman_Family.jpg/520px-Newman_Family.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1154" data-file-height="1130" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Newman_Family.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Witnesses Bill and Gayle Newman dropped to the grass and shielded their children.</div></div></div>
<p>The presidential limousine passed by the <a href="/wiki/Grassy_knoll" class="mw-redirect" title="Grassy knoll">grassy knoll</a> to the north of Elm Street at the time of the fatal head shot. As the motorcade left Dealey Plaza, police officers and spectators ran up the grassy hill and from the triple underpass, to the area behind a five-foot (1.5&#160;m) high stockade fence atop the knoll, separating it from a parking lot. No sniper was found there.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> S. M. Holland, who had been watching the motorcade on the triple underpass, testified that "immediately" after the shots were fired, he saw a puff of smoke arising from the trees right by the stockade fence and then ran around the corner where the overpass joined the fence, but did not see anyone running from that area.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-kenrahn_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kenrahn-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Lee_Bowers" title="Lee Bowers">Lee Bowers</a>, a railroad switchman who was sitting in a two-story tower,<sup id="cite_ref-kenrahn_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kenrahn-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> had an unobstructed view of the rear of the stockade fence atop the grassy knoll during the shooting.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> He saw four men in the area between his tower and Elm Street. That included a middle-aged man and a younger man, standing 10 to 15 feet (3.0 to 4.6&#160;m) apart near the triple underpass, who did not seem to know each other, and one or two uniformed parking lot attendants. At the time of the shooting, he saw "something out of the ordinary, a sort of milling around", which he could not identify. Bowers testified that one or both of the men were still there when motorcycle officer Clyde Haygood ran up the grassy knoll to the back of the fence.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> In a 1966 interview, Bowers clarified that the two men he saw were standing in the opening between the pergola and the fence, and that "no one" was behind the fence at the time the shots were fired.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Meanwhile, <a href="/wiki/Howard_Brennan" title="Howard Brennan">Howard Brennan</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Steamfitter" class="mw-redirect" title="Steamfitter">steamfitter</a> who was sitting across the street from the Texas School Book Depository, notified police that he was watching the motorcade go by when he heard a shot that came from above and looked up to see a man with a rifle take another shot from a corner window on the sixth floor. He said he had seen the same man looking out the window minutes earlier.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Brennan gave a description of the shooter,<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> and Dallas police subsequently broadcast descriptions at 12:45, 12:48, and 12:55&#160;p.m.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> After the second shot was fired, Brennan recalled that "This man [he] saw previous was aiming for his last shot&#160; [...] and maybe paused for another second as though to assure himself that he had hit his mark."<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As Brennan spoke to the police in front of the building, they were joined by Harold Norman and James Jarman, Jr.,<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> two employees of the Texas School Book Depository who had watched the motorcade from windows at the southeast corner of the building's fifth floor.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Norman reported that he heard three gunshots come from directly over their heads.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Norman also heard the sounds of a bolt-action rifle and cartridges dropping on the floor above them.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dallas police sealed off the exits from the Texas School Book Depository range approximately between 12:33 and 12:50&#160;p.m.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There were at least 104 earwitnesses in Dealey Plaza who were on record with an opinion as to the direction from which the shots came. Fifty-four (51.9%) thought that all shots came from the Texas School Book Depository building. Thirty-three (31.7%) thought that they came from either the grassy knoll or the triple underpass. Nine (8.7%) thought that each shot came from a location entirely distinct from the knoll or the depository. Five (4.8%) believed that they heard shots from two locations, and 3 (2.9%) thought that the shots originated from a direction consistent with both the knoll and the depository.<sup id="cite_ref-Earwitnesses_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Earwitnesses-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Warren Commission additionally concluded that three shots were fired and said that "a substantial majority of the witnesses stated that the shots were not evenly spaced. Most witnesses recalled that the second and third shots were bunched together".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._3,_p._110_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._3,_p._110-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lee_Harvey_Oswald_and_Jack_Ruby">Lee Harvey Oswald and Jack Ruby</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lee_Harvey_Oswald" title="Lee Harvey Oswald">Lee Harvey Oswald</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lee_Harvey_Oswald_being_shot_by_Jack_Ruby_as_Oswald_is_being_moved_by_police,_1963.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Lee_Harvey_Oswald_being_shot_by_Jack_Ruby_as_Oswald_is_being_moved_by_police%2C_1963.jpg/240px-Lee_Harvey_Oswald_being_shot_by_Jack_Ruby_as_Oswald_is_being_moved_by_police%2C_1963.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="305" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Lee_Harvey_Oswald_being_shot_by_Jack_Ruby_as_Oswald_is_being_moved_by_police%2C_1963.jpg/360px-Lee_Harvey_Oswald_being_shot_by_Jack_Ruby_as_Oswald_is_being_moved_by_police%2C_1963.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Lee_Harvey_Oswald_being_shot_by_Jack_Ruby_as_Oswald_is_being_moved_by_police%2C_1963.jpg/480px-Lee_Harvey_Oswald_being_shot_by_Jack_Ruby_as_Oswald_is_being_moved_by_police%2C_1963.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2357" data-file-height="3000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lee_Harvey_Oswald_being_shot_by_Jack_Ruby_as_Oswald_is_being_moved_by_police,_1963.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Jack_Ruby" title="Jack Ruby">Jack Ruby</a>, just before firing a single shot into Oswald, who is being escorted by police detectives <a href="/wiki/Jim_Leavelle" title="Jim Leavelle">Jim Leavelle</a> (tan suit) and L.C. Graves for the transfer from the city jail to the county jail</div></div></div>
<p>Roy Truly, Lee Harvey Oswald's supervisor at the depository, reported him missing to the Dallas police.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> About 70&#160;minutes after the assassination, Oswald was arrested for the murder of Dallas police officer <a href="/wiki/J._D._Tippit" title="J. D. Tippit">J. D. Tippit</a>. According to witness Helen Markam, Tippit had spotted Oswald walking along a sidewalk in the residential neighborhood of <a href="/wiki/Oak_Cliff" title="Oak Cliff">Oak Cliff</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Testimony_of_Helen_Markham_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Testimony_of_Helen_Markham-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> three miles from Dealey Plaza. Officer Tippit had earlier received a radio message that gave a description of the suspect being sought in the assassination, and he called Oswald over to the patrol car.
</p><p>Markam testified that after an exchange of words, Tippit got out of his car and Oswald shot him four times.<sup id="cite_ref-Testimony_of_Helen_Markham_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Testimony_of_Helen_Markham-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Multiple witnesses saw a man they identified as Oswald shoot Tippit or flee the scene after emptying the bullet casings from his gun. Oswald was next seen by shoe store manager Johnny Brewer "ducking into" the entrance alcove of his store. Suspicious of this activity, Brewer watched Oswald continue up the street and slip into the nearby <a href="/wiki/Texas_Theatre" title="Texas Theatre">Texas Theatre</a> without paying.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Brewer alerted the theater's ticket clerk, who telephoned the police<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> at about 1:40&#160;p.m.
</p><p>According to M.N. McDonald, who was one of the arresting officers, Oswald resisted arrest and was attempting to draw his pistol when he was struck and forcibly restrained by the police.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> He was charged with the murders of President Kennedy and Officer Tippit later that night.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Oswald denied shooting anyone and claimed he was a <a href="/wiki/Scapegoating" title="Scapegoating">patsy</a> who was arrested because he had lived in the Soviet Union.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Oswald's case never came to trial. Two days after the assassination, as he was being escorted to a car in the basement of Dallas Police Headquarters for the transfer from the city jail to the county jail, Oswald was fatally shot by Dallas nightclub owner <a href="/wiki/Jack_Ruby" title="Jack Ruby">Jack Ruby</a>. The incident was broadcast live on American television at 11:21&#160;a.m. CST on Sunday, November 24. Unconscious, Oswald was rushed by ambulance to <a href="/wiki/Parkland_Memorial_Hospital" title="Parkland Memorial Hospital">Parkland Memorial Hospital</a>, the same facility where doctors had tried to save President Kennedy's life two days earlier; he died at 1:07&#160;p.m.<sup id="cite_ref-Saturday2_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Saturday2-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Oswald's death was announced on a TV news broadcast by Dallas police chief <a href="/wiki/Jesse_Curry" title="Jesse Curry">Jesse Curry</a>. An autopsy was performed by Dallas County Medical Examiner Dr. Earl Rose at 2:45&#160;p.m. the same day. The stated cause of death in the autopsy report was "hemorrhage secondary to gunshot wound of the chest".<sup id="cite_ref-autopsy_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autopsy-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Arrested immediately after the shooting, Ruby later said that he had been distraught over the Kennedy assassination and that killing Oswald would spare "... Mrs. Kennedy the discomfiture of coming back to trial".<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carcano_rifle">Carcano rifle</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy_assassination_rifle" title="John F. Kennedy assassination rifle">John F. Kennedy assassination rifle</a></div>
<p>An Italian <a href="/wiki/Carcano" title="Carcano">Carcano</a> M91/38 bolt-action rifle (see <a href="/wiki/6.5%C3%9752mm_Mannlicher%E2%80%93Carcano" class="mw-redirect" title="6.5×52mm Mannlicher–Carcano">6.5×52mm Mannlicher–Carcano</a> cartridge) was found on the 6th floor of the <a href="/wiki/Texas_School_Book_Depository" title="Texas School Book Depository">Texas School Book Depository</a> by Deputy <a href="/wiki/Constable" title="Constable">Constable</a> Seymour Weitzman and <a href="/wiki/Deputy_Sheriff" class="mw-redirect" title="Deputy Sheriff">Deputy Sheriff</a> Eugene Boone soon after the assassination of President Kennedy.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> The recovery was filmed by Tom Alyea of <a href="/wiki/WFAA-TV" class="mw-redirect" title="WFAA-TV">WFAA-TV</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This footage shows the rifle to be a Carcano, and it was later verified by photographic analysis commissioned by the HSCA that the rifle filmed was the same one later identified as the assassination weapon.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Compared to photographs taken of Oswald holding the rifle in his backyard, "one notch in the stock at [a] point that appears very faintly in the photograph" matched,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._4,_pp._125–126_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._4,_pp._125–126-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> as well as the rifle's dimensions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._4,_p._129_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._4,_p._129-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The secondhand Carcano rifle had been purchased by Oswald in previous March, under the alias "A. Hidell" and delivered to a post office in Dallas where Oswald had rented a <a href="/wiki/Post-office_box" class="mw-redirect" title="Post-office box">post-office box</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._4,_p._118_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._4,_p._118-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> According to the Warren Commission Report, a partial palm print of Oswald was also found on the barrel of the gun,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._4,_p._122_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._4,_p._122-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> and a tuft of fibers found in a crevice of the rifle was consistent with the fibers and colors of the shirt Oswald was wearing at the time of his arrest.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._4,_p._124_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._4,_p._124-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A bullet found on Governor Connally's hospital <a href="/wiki/Gurney" class="mw-redirect" title="Gurney">gurney</a> and two bullet fragments found in the Presidential limousine were <a href="/wiki/Ballistic_fingerprinting" class="mw-redirect" title="Ballistic fingerprinting">ballistically matched</a> to this rifle.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._3,_p._79_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._3,_p._79-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="President_Kennedy_declared_dead_in_the_emergency_room">President Kennedy declared dead in the emergency room</span></h3>
<p>The staff at Parkland Hospital's Trauma Room 1 who treated President Kennedy observed that his condition was moribund, meaning that he had no chance of survival upon arriving at the hospital. George Burkley,<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> the President's personal physician, stated that a gunshot wound to the skull was the cause of death. Burkley signed President Kennedy's death certificate.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lyndon_B._Johnson_taking_the_oath_of_office,_November_1963.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Lyndon_B._Johnson_taking_the_oath_of_office%2C_November_1963.jpg/260px-Lyndon_B._Johnson_taking_the_oath_of_office%2C_November_1963.jpg" decoding="async" width="260" height="206" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Lyndon_B._Johnson_taking_the_oath_of_office%2C_November_1963.jpg/390px-Lyndon_B._Johnson_taking_the_oath_of_office%2C_November_1963.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Lyndon_B._Johnson_taking_the_oath_of_office%2C_November_1963.jpg/520px-Lyndon_B._Johnson_taking_the_oath_of_office%2C_November_1963.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2374" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lyndon_B._Johnson_taking_the_oath_of_office,_November_1963.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cecil_Stoughton" class="mw-redirect" title="Cecil Stoughton">Cecil Stoughton</a>'s iconic photograph of <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">Lyndon B. Johnson</a> being sworn in as U.S. President aboard <a href="/wiki/Air_Force_One" title="Air Force One">Air Force One</a>, Love Field, Dallas. Jacqueline (right), still in her blood-soaked clothes (not visible in picture), looks on.</div></div></div>
<p>The President was pronounced dead at 1:00&#160;p.m., CST (19:00 <a href="/wiki/UTC" class="mw-redirect" title="UTC">UTC</a>) after all heart activity had ceased. Father Oscar Huber<sup id="cite_ref-Southwestern_Historical_Quarterly_2007,_pp._380-393_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Southwestern_Historical_Quarterly_2007,_pp._380-393-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> administered the <a href="/wiki/Last_rites" title="Last rites">last rites</a> of the Roman Catholic Church. Father Huber<sup id="cite_ref-Southwestern_Historical_Quarterly_2007,_pp._380-393_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Southwestern_Historical_Quarterly_2007,_pp._380-393-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> told <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> that the President was already dead by the time he arrived at the hospital, and he had to draw back a sheet covering the President's face to administer the sacrament of <a href="/wiki/Anointing_of_the_Sick_(Catholic_Church)" class="mw-redirect" title="Anointing of the Sick (Catholic Church)">Extreme Unction</a>. President Kennedy's death was officially announced by White House Acting Press Secretary <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_Kilduff" title="Malcolm Kilduff">Malcolm Kilduff</a> at 1:33&#160;p.m. CST (19:33 <a href="/wiki/UTC" class="mw-redirect" title="UTC">UTC</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Kilduff was acting press secretary on the trip because <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Salinger" title="Pierre Salinger">Pierre Salinger</a> was traveling to Japan with half the Cabinet, including Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Dean_Rusk" title="Dean Rusk">Dean Rusk</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Governor Connally, meanwhile, was taken to emergency surgery, where he underwent two operations that day.
</p><p>Members of the President's security detail were attempting to remove Kennedy's body from the hospital when they briefly scuffled with Dallas officials, including Dallas County Coroner <a href="/wiki/Earl_Rose_(coroner)" title="Earl Rose (coroner)">Earl Rose</a>, who believed that he was legally obligated to perform an autopsy before the President's body was removed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBugliosi200792f–93f_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBugliosi200792f–93f-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> The Secret Service pushed through and Rose eventually stepped aside.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBugliosi2007110–111_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBugliosi2007110–111-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> The forensic panel of the HSCA, of which Rose was a member, later reported that Texas law indicated that it was the responsibility of the <a href="/wiki/Justice_of_the_peace" title="Justice of the peace">justice of the peace</a> to determine the cause of death as well as the necessity of whether an autopsy was needed to determine the cause of death.<sup id="cite_ref-HCSA-VII_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HCSA-VII-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Theran Ward, a justice of the peace in Dallas County, signed the official record of inquest<sup id="cite_ref-HCSA-VII_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HCSA-VII-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> as well as a second certificate of death.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A few minutes after 2:00&#160;p.m. CST (20:00 <a href="/wiki/UTC" class="mw-redirect" title="UTC">UTC</a>), Kennedy's body was taken from Parkland Hospital to Love Field. His casket was then loaded onto Air Force One through the rear door, where it remained at the rear of the passenger compartment in place of a removed row of seats.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Johnson had accompanied Kennedy to Dallas and was riding two cars behind the President in the motorcade. The new President refused to leave for Washington without Kennedy's remains and his widow.
</p><p>At 2:38&#160;p.m. CST (20:38 <a href="/wiki/UTC" class="mw-redirect" title="UTC">UTC</a>), Lyndon Johnson, with Jacqueline Kennedy at his side, <a href="/wiki/First_inauguration_of_Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="First inauguration of Lyndon B. Johnson">took the oath of office</a> administered by federal judge <a href="/wiki/Sarah_T._Hughes" title="Sarah T. Hughes">Sarah T. Hughes</a> on board Air Force One shortly before departing from Love Field for the flight back to Washington, D.C.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Autopsy">Autopsy</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy_autopsy" title="John F. Kennedy autopsy">John F. Kennedy autopsy</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy_autopsy" title="John F. Kennedy autopsy">autopsy</a> was performed at the <a href="/wiki/National_Naval_Medical_Center" class="mw-redirect" title="National Naval Medical Center">Bethesda Naval Hospital</a> in Bethesda, <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a>; the procedure began at about 8&#160;p.m. and ended at about midnight EST. The choice of autopsy hospital in the Washington, D.C., area was made at the request of Mrs. Kennedy, on the basis that John F. Kennedy had been a <a href="/wiki/Naval_officer" class="mw-redirect" title="Naval officer">naval officer</a> during World War II.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Funeral">Funeral</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/State_funeral_of_John_F._Kennedy" title="State funeral of John F. Kennedy">State funeral of John F. Kennedy</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/State_funeral_of_John_F._Kennedy" title="State funeral of John F. Kennedy">state funeral</a> took place in Washington, D.C., during the three days that followed the assassination.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The body of President Kennedy was flown back to Washington, D.C., and placed in the <a href="/wiki/East_Room" title="East Room">East Room of the White House</a> for 24 hours.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> On the Sunday after the assassination, his coffin was carried on a horse-drawn <a href="/wiki/Limbers_and_caissons" title="Limbers and caissons">caisson</a> to the United States <a href="/wiki/United_States_Capitol" title="United States Capitol">Capitol</a> to <a href="/wiki/Lying_in_state" title="Lying in state">lie in state</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Throughout the day and night, hundreds of thousands of people lined up to view the guarded casket.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Representatives from over 90 countries attended the state funeral on Monday, November 25.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/Requiem_Mass" class="mw-redirect" title="Requiem Mass">Requiem Mass</a> at <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_of_St._Matthew_the_Apostle" class="mw-redirect" title="Cathedral of St. Matthew the Apostle">St. Matthew's Cathedral</a>, the President was laid to rest 2.7 miles from the White House at <a href="/wiki/Arlington_National_Cemetery" title="Arlington National Cemetery">Arlington National Cemetery</a> in Virginia.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recordings_of_the_assassination">Recordings of the assassination</span></h2>
<p>No radio or television stations broadcast the assassination live. Most media crews did not ride with the motorcade, but were instead waiting at the Dallas Trade Mart in anticipation of President Kennedy's arrival there. Members of the media who were with the motorcade were riding at the rear of the procession.
</p><p>The Dallas police were recording their radio transmissions over two individual channels. A frequency designated as Channel One was used for routine police communications, while Channel Two was an auxiliary channel dedicated to the President's motorcade. Up until the time of the assassination, most of the broadcasts on the second channel consisted of Police Chief Jesse Curry's announcements of the location of the motorcade as it traveled through the city.
</p><p>President Kennedy's last seconds of traveling through Dealey Plaza were recorded on silent <a href="/wiki/8_mm_film" title="8 mm film">8 mm film</a> for the 26.6&#160;seconds before, during, and immediately following the assassination. This famous film footage was taken by garment manufacturer and amateur cameraman <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Zapruder" title="Abraham Zapruder">Abraham Zapruder</a>, and became known as the <b><a href="/wiki/Zapruder_film" title="Zapruder film">Zapruder film</a></b>. Frame enlargements from the Zapruder film were published by <a href="/wiki/Life_(magazine)" title="Life (magazine)"><i>Life</i> magazine</a> shortly after the assassination. The footage was first shown publicly as a film at the <a href="/wiki/Trial_of_Clay_Shaw" title="Trial of Clay Shaw">trial of Clay Shaw</a> in 1969, and on television in 1975.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> According to the <i>Guinness Book of World Records</i>, in 1999 an arbitration panel ordered the United States government to pay $615,384 per second of film to Zapruder's heirs for giving the film to the National Archives. The complete film, which lasts for roughly over 26&#160;seconds, was valued at $16 million.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Zapruder was not the only person who photographed at least part of the assassination; a total of 32 photographers were in Dealey Plaza that day. Amateur movies taken by <a href="/wiki/Orville_Nix" title="Orville Nix">Orville Nix</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marie_Muchmore" title="Marie Muchmore">Marie Muchmore</a> (shown on television in New York on November 26, 1963),<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> and photographer Charles Bronson captured the fatal shot, although at a greater distance than Zapruder did. Other motion picture films were taken in Dealey Plaza at or around the time of the shooting by Robert Hughes, F. Mark Bell, Elsie Dorman, John Martin Jr., Patsy Paschall, Tina Towner, James Underwood, Dave Wiegman, <a href="/wiki/Mal_Couch" title="Mal Couch">Mal Couch</a>, Thomas Atkins, and an unknown woman in a blue dress on the south side of Elm Street.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Still photos were taken by <a href="/wiki/Phillip_Willis" title="Phillip Willis">Phillip Willis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mary_Moorman" title="Mary Moorman">Mary Moorman</a>, Hugh W. Betzner Jr., Wilma Bond, Robert Croft, and many others. <a href="/wiki/Ike_Altgens" title="Ike Altgens">Ike Altgens</a> was the lone professional photographer in Dealey Plaza who was not in the press cars; he was a photo editor for the <a href="/wiki/Associated_Press" title="Associated Press">Associated Press</a> in Dallas.
</p><p>An unidentified woman, nicknamed by researchers as the <b><a href="/wiki/Babushka_Lady" title="Babushka Lady">Babushka Lady</a></b>, might have been filming the Presidential motorcade during the assassination. She was seen apparently doing so on film and in photographs taken by the others.
</p><p>Previously unknown color footage filmed on the assassination day by George Jefferies was released on February 19, 2007, by the Sixth Floor Museum.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> The film does not include the actual shooting, having been taken over 90&#160;seconds beforehand and a couple of blocks away. The only detail relevant to the investigation of the assassination is a clear view of President Kennedy's bunched suit jacket, just below the collar, which has led to different calculations about how low in the back President Kennedy was first shot (see discussion above).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Official_investigations">Official investigations</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dallas_Police">Dallas Police</span></h3>
<p>After the Dallas Police arrested Oswald and collected physical evidence at the crime scenes, they held Oswald at their headquarters for interrogation. All afternoon, they asked Oswald about the Tippit shooting and the assassination of the President. They intermittently questioned him for approximately 12&#160;hours between 2:30&#160;p.m., on November 22, and 11&#160;a.m., on November 24.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._4,_p._180_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._4,_p._180-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Throughout this interrogation, Oswald denied any involvement with either Kennedy's assassination or Patrolman Tippit's murder.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._4,_p._180_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._4,_p._180-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Captain Fritz of the homicide and robbery bureau did most of the questioning and kept only rudimentary notes.<sup id="cite_ref-fritz_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fritz-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> Days later, he wrote a report of the interrogation from notes he made afterwards.<sup id="cite_ref-fritz_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fritz-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> There were no stenographic or tape recordings. Representatives of other law enforcement agencies were also present, including the <a href="/wiki/FBI" class="mw-redirect" title="FBI">FBI</a> and the Secret Service, and occasionally participated in the questioning.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._4,_pp._180–195_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._4,_pp._180–195-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Several of the FBI agents who were present wrote contemporaneous reports of the interrogation.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On the evening of the assassination, Dallas Police performed <a href="/wiki/Paraffin_test" class="mw-redirect" title="Paraffin test">paraffin tests</a> on Oswald's hands and right cheek in an apparent effort to determine, by means of a scientific test, whether or not he had recently fired a weapon.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._4,_pp._180–195_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._4,_pp._180–195-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> The results were positive for the hands and negative for the right cheek.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._4,_pp._180–195_121-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._4,_pp._180–195-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> These tests were unreliable, and the Warren Commission did not rely on the results of the test in making their findings.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._4,_pp._180–195_121-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._4,_pp._180–195-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Oswald provided little information during his questioning by police. When confronted with evidence that he could not explain, he resorted to statements that were found to be false.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._4,_pp._180–195_121-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren1964chpt._4,_pp._180–195-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="FBI_investigation">FBI investigation</span></h3>
<p>The FBI was the first authority to complete an investigation. On December 9, 1963, the FBI issued a report and gave it to the Warren Commission.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The FBI stated that three bullets were fired during the Kennedy assassination; the Warren Commission agreed with the FBI investigation that three shots were fired but disagreed with the FBI report on which shots hit Kennedy and which hit Governor Connally. The FBI report claimed that the first shot hit President Kennedy, the second shot hit Governor Connally, and the third shot hit President Kennedy in the head, killing him. In contrast, the Warren Commission concluded that one of the three shots missed, one of the shots hit President Kennedy and then struck Governor Connally, and a third shot struck President Kennedy in the head, killing him.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Warren_Commission">Warren Commission</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Warren_Commission_presenting_report_on_assassination_of_John_F._Kennedy_to_Lyndon_Johnson.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Warren_Commission_presenting_report_on_assassination_of_John_F._Kennedy_to_Lyndon_Johnson.jpg/260px-Warren_Commission_presenting_report_on_assassination_of_John_F._Kennedy_to_Lyndon_Johnson.jpg" decoding="async" width="260" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Warren_Commission_presenting_report_on_assassination_of_John_F._Kennedy_to_Lyndon_Johnson.jpg/390px-Warren_Commission_presenting_report_on_assassination_of_John_F._Kennedy_to_Lyndon_Johnson.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Warren_Commission_presenting_report_on_assassination_of_John_F._Kennedy_to_Lyndon_Johnson.jpg/520px-Warren_Commission_presenting_report_on_assassination_of_John_F._Kennedy_to_Lyndon_Johnson.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2379" data-file-height="1833" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Warren_Commission_presenting_report_on_assassination_of_John_F._Kennedy_to_Lyndon_Johnson.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Warren_Commission" title="Warren Commission">Warren Commission</a> presents its report to President Johnson. From left to right: <a href="/wiki/John_J._McCloy" title="John J. McCloy">John McCloy</a>, <a href="/wiki/J._Lee_Rankin" title="J. Lee Rankin">J. Lee Rankin</a> (General Counsel), Senator <a href="/wiki/Richard_Russell,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Richard Russell, Jr.">Richard Russell</a>, Congressman <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Ford" title="Gerald Ford">Gerald Ford</a>, Chief Justice <a href="/wiki/Earl_Warren" title="Earl Warren">Earl Warren</a>, President <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">Lyndon B. Johnson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Allen_Dulles" title="Allen Dulles">Allen Dulles</a>, Senator <a href="/wiki/John_Sherman_Cooper" title="John Sherman Cooper">John Sherman Cooper</a>, and Congressman <a href="/wiki/Hale_Boggs" title="Hale Boggs">Hale Boggs</a>.</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Warren_Commission" title="Warren Commission">Warren Commission</a></div>
<p>The President's Commission on the Assassination of President Kennedy, known unofficially as the Warren Commission, was established on November 29, 1963, by President Johnson to investigate the assassination.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> Its 888-page final report was presented to Johnson on September 24, 1964,<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> and made public three days later.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> It concluded that <a href="/wiki/Lee_Harvey_Oswald" title="Lee Harvey Oswald">Lee Harvey Oswald</a> acted alone in the killing of President Kennedy and the wounding of Texas <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Texas" title="Governor of Texas">Governor</a> <a href="/wiki/John_Connally" title="John Connally">John Connally</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> and that <a href="/wiki/Jack_Ruby" title="Jack Ruby">Jack Ruby</a> also acted alone in the murder of Oswald.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> The Commission's findings and conclusions have since proven controversial and been both criticized and supported by later studies.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Commission took its unofficial name, "The Warren Commission", from its chairman, <a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_of_the_United_States" title="Chief Justice of the United States">Chief Justice</a> <a href="/wiki/Earl_Warren" title="Earl Warren">Earl Warren</a>. According to published transcripts of Johnson's presidential phone conversations, some major officials were opposed to forming such a commission, and several commission members took part only with extreme reluctance.<sup id="cite_ref-Conversations_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Conversations-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> One of their chief reservations was that a commission would ultimately create more controversy than consensus, and those fears ultimately proved valid.<sup id="cite_ref-Conversations_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Conversations-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>All of the Warren Commission's records were submitted to the <a href="/wiki/National_Archives_and_Records_Administration" title="National Archives and Records Administration">National Archives</a> in 1964. The unpublished portion of those records was initially sealed for 75&#160;years (to 2039) under a general National Archives policy that applied to all federal investigations by the executive branch of government,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBugliosi2007136–137_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBugliosi2007136–137-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> a period "intended to serve as protection for innocent persons who could otherwise be damaged because of their relationship with participants in the case".<sup id="cite_ref-Bahmer24_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bahmer24-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> The 75-year rule no longer exists, supplanted by the <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_Information_Act_(United_States)" title="Freedom of Information Act (United States)">Freedom of Information Act</a> of 1966 and the <a href="/wiki/President_John_F._Kennedy_Assassination_Records_Collection_Act_of_1992" title="President John F. Kennedy Assassination Records Collection Act of 1992">JFK Records Act</a> of 1992.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ramsey_Clark_Panel">Ramsey Clark Panel</span></h3>
<p>In 1968, a panel of four medical experts appointed by <a href="/wiki/United_States_Attorney_General" title="United States Attorney General">Attorney General</a> <a href="/wiki/Ramsey_Clark" title="Ramsey Clark">Ramsey Clark</a> met in Washington, D.C., to examine various photographs, X-ray films, documents, and other evidence about the death of President Kennedy. The Clark Panel determined that President Kennedy was struck by two bullets fired from above and behind him, one of which traversed the base of the neck on the right side without striking bone and the other of which entered the skull from behind and destroyed its upper right side. The report also indicates that the skull shot entered well above the external occipital protuberance, which was at odds with the Warren Commission's findings.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rockefeller_Commission">Rockefeller Commission</span></h3>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/United_States_President%27s_Commission_on_CIA_activities_within_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="United States President&#39;s Commission on CIA activities within the United States">United States President's Commission on CIA activities within the United States</a></i> was set up under President <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Ford" title="Gerald Ford">Gerald Ford</a> in 1975 to investigate the activities of the <a href="/wiki/Central_Intelligence_Agency" title="Central Intelligence Agency">CIA</a> within the United States. The commission was led by Vice President <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Rockefeller" title="Nelson Rockefeller">Nelson Rockefeller</a>, and is sometimes referred to as the Rockefeller Commission.
</p><p>Part of the commission's work dealt with the Kennedy assassination, specifically the head snap as seen in the <a href="/wiki/Zapruder_film" title="Zapruder film">Zapruder film</a> (first shown to the general public in 1975), and the possible presence of <a href="/wiki/E._Howard_Hunt" title="E. Howard Hunt">E. Howard Hunt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frank_Sturgis" title="Frank Sturgis">Frank Sturgis</a> in Dallas.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> The commission concluded that neither Hunt nor Sturgis was in Dallas at the time of the assassination.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Church_Committee">Church Committee</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Church_Committee" title="Church Committee">Church Committee</a> is the common term referring to the 1975 <i>United States Senate Select Committee to Study Governmental Operations with Respect to Intelligence Activities</i>, a <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">U.S. Senate</a> committee chaired by Senator <a href="/wiki/Frank_Church" title="Frank Church">Frank Church</a>, to investigate the illegal intelligence gathering by the <a href="/wiki/Central_Intelligence_Agency" title="Central Intelligence Agency">Central Intelligence Agency</a> (CIA) and <a href="/wiki/Federal_Bureau_of_Investigation" title="Federal Bureau of Investigation">Federal Bureau of Investigation</a> (FBI) after the <a href="/wiki/Watergate_scandal" title="Watergate scandal">Watergate</a> incident. It also investigated the CIA and FBI conduct relating to the JFK assassination.
</p><p>Their report concluded that the investigation on the assassination by FBI and CIA were fundamentally deficient and that facts that may have greatly affected the investigation had not been forwarded to the Warren Commission by the agencies. The report hinted that there was a possibility that senior officials in both agencies made conscious decisions not to disclose potentially important information.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States_House_Select_Committee_on_Assassinations">United States House Select Committee on Assassinations</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Select_Committee_on_Assassinations" title="United States House Select Committee on Assassinations">United States House Select Committee on Assassinations</a></div>
<p>As a result of increasing public and congressional skepticism regarding the Warren Commission's findings and the transparency of government agencies, House Resolution 1540 was passed in September 1976, creating the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Select_Committee_on_Assassinations" title="United States House Select Committee on Assassinations">United States House Select Committee on Assassinations</a> (HSCA) to investigate the assassinations of President Kennedy and <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Martin_Luther_King,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Assassination of Martin Luther King, Jr.">Martin Luther King, Jr.</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStokes19799-16_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStokes19799-16-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Committee investigated until 1978, and in March 1979 issued its final report, concluding that President John F.
